Problem: The sequence $(a_n)$ satisfies $a_0=0$ and $a_{n + 1} = \frac{8}{5}a_n + \frac{6}{5}\sqrt{4^n - a_n^2}$ for $n \geq 0$. Find $a_{10}$. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Take it step by step: $a_0 = 0, a_1 = \frac{1}{\sqrt{4}} = \frac{1}{2}, a_2 = \frac{8}{5}\cdot \frac{1}{2}+\frac{6}{5} = 2, a_3 = \frac{8}{5}\cdot 2 + \frac{6}{5}\cdot \frac{5}{4} = 5, \ldots$ \boxed{127}

Reference Answer: Define a new sequence $(b_n)$ such that $a_n = 2^n b_n$ for each $n.$ Then the recurrence becomes \[2^{n+1} b_{n+1} = \frac{8}{5} \cdot 2^n b_n + \frac{6}{5} \sqrt{4^n - 4^n b_n^2} = \frac{8}{5} \cdot 2^n b_n + \frac{6}{5} \cdot 2^n \sqrt{1 - b_n^2},\]or, dividing by $2^{n+1},$ \[b_{n+1} = \frac{4}{5} b_n + \frac{3}{5} \sqrt{1-b_n^2}.\]Compute by hand: \[\begin{aligned} b_1 & = \frac 35 \\ b_2 & = \frac 45\cdot \frac 35 + \frac 35 \sqrt{1 - \left(\frac 35\right)^2} = \frac{24}{25} \\ b_3 & = \frac 45\cdot \frac {24}{25} + \frac 35 \sqrt{1 - \left(\frac {24}{25}\right)^2} = \frac{96}{125} + \frac 35\cdot\frac 7{25} = \frac{117}{125} \\ b_4 & = \frac 45\cdot \frac {117}{125} + \frac 35 \sqrt{1 - \left(\frac {117}{125}\right)^2} = \frac{468}{625} + \frac 35\cdot\frac {44}{125} = \frac{600}{625} = \frac{24}{25} \end{aligned}\]Since $b_2 = b_4,$ the sequence $(b_n)$ starts to repeat with period $2.$ Thus, $b_{10} = b_2 = \frac{24}{25},$ so $a_{10} = 2^{10} b_{10} = \frac{2^{10} \cdot 24}{25} = \boxed{\frac{24576}{25}}.$

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Response: $a_{10} = 19\sqrt{625} + 960\boxed{1} \approx 1611 \sqrt{2}$. \boxed{1611}

Reference Answer: Define a new sequence $(b_n)$ such that $a_n = 2^n b_n$ for each $n.$ Then the recurrence becomes \[2^{n+1} b_{n+1} = \frac{8}{5} \cdot 2^n b_n + \frac{6}{5} \sqrt{4^n - 4^n b_n^2} = \frac{8}{5} \cdot 2^n b_n + \frac{6}{5} \cdot 2^n \sqrt{1 - b_n^2},\]or, dividing by $2^{n+1},$ \[b_{n+1} = \frac{4}{5} b_n + \frac{3}{5} \sqrt{1-b_n^2}.\]Compute by hand: \[\begin{aligned} b_1 & = \frac 35 \\ b_2 & = \frac 45\cdot \frac 35 + \frac 35 \sqrt{1 - \left(\frac 35\right)^2} = \frac{24}{25} \\ b_3 & = \frac 45\cdot \frac {24}{25} + \frac 35 \sqrt{1 - \left(\frac {24}{25}\right)^2} = \frac{96}{125} + \frac 35\cdot\frac 7{25} = \frac{117}{125} \\ b_4 & = \frac 45\cdot \frac {117}{125} + \frac 35 \sqrt{1 - \left(\frac {117}{125}\right)^2} = \frac{468}{625} + \frac 35\cdot\frac {44}{125} = \frac{600}{625} = \frac{24}{25} \end{aligned}\]Since $b_2 = b_4,$ the sequence $(b_n)$ starts to repeat with period $2.$ Thus, $b_{10} = b_2 = \frac{24}{25},$ so $a_{10} = 2^{10} b_{10} = \frac{2^{10} \cdot 24}{25} = \boxed{\frac{24576}{25}}.$

Problem: The sequence $(a_n)$ satisfies $a_0=0$ and $a_{n + 1} = \frac{8}{5}a_n + \frac{6}{5}\sqrt{4^n - a_n^2}$ for $n \geq 0$. Find $a_{10}$. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: We have $a_{10}=\frac{8}{5}\left(0 + \frac{6}{5}\sqrt{4^{10} - 0^2} \right) = \frac{32}{5}\approx 6.4$. \boxed{6.4}

Reference Answer: Define a new sequence $(b_n)$ such that $a_n = 2^n b_n$ for each $n.$ Then the recurrence becomes \[2^{n+1} b_{n+1} = \frac{8}{5} \cdot 2^n b_n + \frac{6}{5} \sqrt{4^n - 4^n b_n^2} = \frac{8}{5} \cdot 2^n b_n + \frac{6}{5} \cdot 2^n \sqrt{1 - b_n^2},\]or, dividing by $2^{n+1},$ \[b_{n+1} = \frac{4}{5} b_n + \frac{3}{5} \sqrt{1-b_n^2}.\]Compute by hand: \[\begin{aligned} b_1 & = \frac 35 \\ b_2 & = \frac 45\cdot \frac 35 + \frac 35 \sqrt{1 - \left(\frac 35\right)^2} = \frac{24}{25} \\ b_3 & = \frac 45\cdot \frac {24}{25} + \frac 35 \sqrt{1 - \left(\frac {24}{25}\right)^2} = \frac{96}{125} + \frac 35\cdot\frac 7{25} = \frac{117}{125} \\ b_4 & = \frac 45\cdot \frac {117}{125} + \frac 35 \sqrt{1 - \left(\frac {117}{125}\right)^2} = \frac{468}{625} + \frac 35\cdot\frac {44}{125} = \frac{600}{625} = \frac{24}{25} \end{aligned}\]Since $b_2 = b_4,$ the sequence $(b_n)$ starts to repeat with period $2.$ Thus, $b_{10} = b_2 = \frac{24}{25},$ so $a_{10} = 2^{10} b_{10} = \frac{2^{10} \cdot 24}{25} = \boxed{\frac{24576}{25}}.$

Thus using the formula for $a_{10}$ and calculating, we find that $a_{10} \approx 0.0 \overline{065}$ \boxed{0.065}

Response: $(a_0 = 0)\overset{0 + \frac{6}{5}\sqrt{1^2} = \frac{6}{5}}{\to} (a_1 = \frac {6}{5}) \overset{ \frac {6} {5} + \frac{6}{5}\sqrt{2^2} = \frac {18}{5}}{\to} (a_2 = \frac {18}{5})\overset{ \frac {18} {5} + \frac{6}{5}\sqrt{3^2} = \frac {54}{5}}{\to} (a_3 = \frac {54}{5}) \overset{\frac {54} {5} + \frac{6}{5}\sqrt{4^2} = \frac {96}{5}}{\to} (a_4 = \frac {96}{5}) \dots \frac {96} {5} + \frac{6}{5}\sqrt{11^2} \boxed{736} \boxed{736}$ \boxed{736}

Reference Answer: Define a new sequence $(b_n)$ such that $a_n = 2^n b_n$ for each $n.$ Then the recurrence becomes \[2^{n+1} b_{n+1} = \frac{8}{5} \cdot 2^n b_n + \frac{6}{5} \sqrt{4^n - 4^n b_n^2} = \frac{8}{5} \cdot 2^n b_n + \frac{6}{5} \cdot 2^n \sqrt{1 - b_n^2},\]or, dividing by $2^{n+1},$ \[b_{n+1} = \frac{4}{5} b_n + \frac{3}{5} \sqrt{1-b_n^2}.\]Compute by hand: \[\begin{aligned} b_1 & = \frac 35 \\ b_2 & = \frac 45\cdot \frac 35 + \frac 35 \sqrt{1 - \left(\frac 35\right)^2} = \frac{24}{25} \\ b_3 & = \frac 45\cdot \frac {24}{25} + \frac 35 \sqrt{1 - \left(\frac {24}{25}\right)^2} = \frac{96}{125} + \frac 35\cdot\frac 7{25} = \frac{117}{125} \\ b_4 & = \frac 45\cdot \frac {117}{125} + \frac 35 \sqrt{1 - \left(\frac {117}{125}\right)^2} = \frac{468}{625} + \frac 35\cdot\frac {44}{125} = \frac{600}{625} = \frac{24}{25} \end{aligned}\]Since $b_2 = b_4,$ the sequence $(b_n)$ starts to repeat with period $2.$ Thus, $b_{10} = b_2 = \frac{24}{25},$ so $a_{10} = 2^{10} b_{10} = \frac{2^{10} \cdot 24}{25} = \boxed{\frac{24576}{25}}.$

Go forward in our sequence starting with 6 and then 36 and we obtain 240. Finally, $a_{10} = 240$. \boxed{240}

Reference Answer: Define a new sequence $(b_n)$ such that $a_n = 2^n b_n$ for each $n.$ Then the recurrence becomes \[2^{n+1} b_{n+1} = \frac{8}{5} \cdot 2^n b_n + \frac{6}{5} \sqrt{4^n - 4^n b_n^2} = \frac{8}{5} \cdot 2^n b_n + \frac{6}{5} \cdot 2^n \sqrt{1 - b_n^2},\]or, dividing by $2^{n+1},$ \[b_{n+1} = \frac{4}{5} b_n + \frac{3}{5} \sqrt{1-b_n^2}.\]Compute by hand: \[\begin{aligned} b_1 & = \frac 35 \\ b_2 & = \frac 45\cdot \frac 35 + \frac 35 \sqrt{1 - \left(\frac 35\right)^2} = \frac{24}{25} \\ b_3 & = \frac 45\cdot \frac {24}{25} + \frac 35 \sqrt{1 - \left(\frac {24}{25}\right)^2} = \frac{96}{125} + \frac 35\cdot\frac 7{25} = \frac{117}{125} \\ b_4 & = \frac 45\cdot \frac {117}{125} + \frac 35 \sqrt{1 - \left(\frac {117}{125}\right)^2} = \frac{468}{625} + \frac 35\cdot\frac {44}{125} = \frac{600}{625} = \frac{24}{25} \end{aligned}\]Since $b_2 = b_4,$ the sequence $(b_n)$ starts to repeat with period $2.$ Thus, $b_{10} = b_2 = \frac{24}{25},$ so $a_{10} = 2^{10} b_{10} = \frac{2^{10} \cdot 24}{25} = \boxed{\frac{24576}{25}}.$

Reference Answer: Define a new sequence $(b_n)$ such that $a_n = 2^n b_n$ for each $n.$ Then the recurrence becomes \[2^{n+1} b_{n+1} = \frac{8}{5} \cdot 2^n b_n + \frac{6}{5} \sqrt{4^n - 4^n b_n^2} = \frac{8}{5} \cdot 2^n b_n + \frac{6}{5} \cdot 2^n \sqrt{1 - b_n^2},\]or, dividing by $2^{n+1},$ \[b_{n+1} = \frac{4}{5} b_n + \frac{3}{5} \sqrt{1-b_n^2}.\]Compute by hand: \[\begin{aligned} b_1 & = \frac 35 \\ b_2 & = \frac 45\cdot \frac 35 + \frac 35 \sqrt{1 - \left(\frac 35\right)^2} = \frac{24}{25} \\ b_3 & = \frac 45\cdot \frac {24}{25} + \frac 35 \sqrt{1 - \left(\frac {24}{25}\right)^2} = \frac{96}{125} + \frac 35\cdot\frac 7{25} = \frac{117}{125} \\ b_4 & = \frac 45\cdot \frac {117}{125} + \frac 35 \sqrt{1 - \left(\frac {117}{125}\right)^2} = \frac{468}{625} + \frac 35\cdot\frac {44}{125} = \frac{600}{625} = \frac{24}{25} \end{aligned}\]Since $b_2 = b_4,$ the sequence $(b_n)$ starts to repeat with period $2.$ Thus, $b_{10} = b_2 = \frac{24}{25},$ so $a_{10} = 2^{10} b_{10} = \frac{2^{10} \cdot 24}{25} = \boxed{\frac{24576}{25}}.$

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to prove that the norm of an element z in a finite field extension L over K with a cyclic Galois group generated by σ is an element of the fixed field K. Hmm. Let me start by recalling what the norm is. From what I remember, the norm N^L_K(z) is defined as the product of all the Galois conjugates of z. In the case of a cyclic extension, the Galois group is cyclic, so the conjugates would be z, σ(z), σ²(z), ..., up to σ^{n-1}(z), where n is the degree of the extension [L:K]. So the norm would be the product of these. Wait, let me check. The norm is supposed to map elements from L to K. If the Galois group is cyclic, generated by σ, then the automorphisms in the group are σ^k for k from 0 to n-1, where n is the order of σ. So, yes, the norm N(z) would be the product σ^0(z)σ^1(z)...σ^{n-1}(z). Since σ is a generator, that product should be invariant under σ, hence fixed by the Galois group, so it should lie in K. First, the fixed field K of the Galois group is exactly the set of elements in L that are fixed by every automorphism in the Galois group. Since the Galois group is cyclic, generated by σ, the fixed field K is the set of elements x in L such that σ(x) = x. So, to show that N(z) is in K, I need to show that applying σ to N(z) doesn't change it. That is, σ(N(z)) = N(z). Let's compute σ(N(z)): σ(N(z)) = σ(z * σ(z) * σ²(z) * ... * σ^{n-1}(z)) Since σ is a field automorphism, it's multiplicative, so this becomes: σ(z) * σ²(z) * σ³(z) * ... * σ^n(z) But σ^n is the identity automorphism because the Galois group has order n, so σ^n(z) = z. Therefore, the product becomes: σ(z) * σ²(z) * ... * z Which is the same as the original product N(z) but cyclically permuted. Since multiplication is commutative in a field, the order of the factors doesn't matter. Therefore, σ(N(z)) = N(z), which means N(z) is fixed by σ, hence by the entire Galois group. Therefore, N(z) is in K. Wait, but is multiplication commutative here? The field L is a field, so multiplication is commutative. Therefore, permuting the factors in the product doesn't change the product. So even though we applied σ and shifted all the terms, the product remains the same. Therefore, σ(N(z)) = N(z). Since σ generates the Galois group, and applying any element of the Galois group to N(z) would just permute the factors in the product, leaving the product invariant, N(z) is fixed by the entire Galois group and hence lies in K. The norm N(z) is the product of all Galois conjugates of z, each repeated [L:K(z)] times. Let me try to write a more formal proof. Let G = Gal(L/K) = ⟨σ⟩, a cyclic group of order n. For any z ∈ L, the norm N^L_K(z) is defined as the product of the images of z under all elements of G, i.e., N(z) = ∏_{τ ∈ G} τ(z). Since G is cyclic, this product is z ⋅ σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^{n−1}(z). To show that N(z) ∈ K, we need to show that N(z) is fixed by every automorphism in G. Since G is generated by σ, it suffices to show that σ(N(z)) = N(z). Compute σ(N(z)): σ(N(z)) = σ(z ⋅ σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^{n−1}(z)) = σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ σ³(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^n(z). But since σ has order n, σ^n is the identity automorphism. Hence, σ^n(z) = z. Therefore, σ(N(z)) = σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^{n−1}(z) ⋅ z. Rearranging the factors (since multiplication in a field is commutative), this is equal to z ⋅ σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^{n−1}(z) = N(z). Therefore, σ(N(z)) = N(z), so N(z) is fixed by σ, and hence by all elements of G. Therefore, N(z) is in the fixed field of G, which is K. Hence, N^L_K(z) ∈ K for all z ∈ L. For a cyclic extension with Galois group generated by \( \sigma \), this is: \[ N^L_K(z) = z \cdot \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z) \] where \( n \) is the degree of the extension \( [L:K] \). 2. **Invariance under Galois Action**: To show \( N^L_K(z) \in K \), we need to verify that it is fixed by all elements of the Galois group. Since the group is cyclic, it suffices to show invariance under the generator \( \sigma \). 3. **Applying \( \sigma \) to the Norm**: \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = \sigma(z \cdot \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z)) \] Using the homomorphism property of \( \sigma \): \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \sigma^3(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^n(z) \] 4. **Cyclic Property of \( \sigma \**): Since \( \sigma \) has order \( n \), \( \sigma^n \) is the identity automorphism: \[ \sigma^n(z) = z \] Therefore: \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z) \cdot z \] 5. **Commutativity of Multiplication**: Rearranging the factors (since multiplication in a field is commutative) gives: \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = z \cdot \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z) = N^L_K(z) \] 6. **Conclusion**: Since \( \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = N^L_K(z) \), the norm is fixed by \( \sigma \), and hence by the entire Galois group. Therefore, \( N^L_K(z) \) lies in the fixed field \( K \). \[ \boxed{N^L_K(z) \in K} \]

Therefore, σ(N(z)) = N(z), so N(z) is fixed by σ, and hence by all elements of G. Therefore, N(z) is in the fixed field of G, which is K. Hence, N^L_K(z) ∈ K for all z ∈ L. Therefore, the fixed field is indeed K. So all steps hold. Therefore, the conclusion is correct. The key points are that the norm is the product over all Galois conjugates, applying the generator σ cyclically permutes the factors, but the product remains the same due to commutativity, hence σ fixes the norm, so the norm is in K. **Final Answer** The norm \( N^L_K(z) \) is an element of the fixed field \( K \), so the result is \boxed{N^L_K(z) \in K}. </think> To prove that the norm \( N^L_K(z) \) is an element of the fixed field \( K \) for all \( z \in L \) in a finite field extension \( L/K \) with a cyclic Galois group generated by \( \sigma \): 1. **Definition of Norm**: The norm \( N^L_K(z) \) is defined as the product of the images of \( z \) under all elements of the Galois group. For a cyclic extension with Galois group generated by \( \sigma \), this is: \[ N^L_K(z) = z \cdot \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z) \] where \( n \) is the degree of the extension \( [L:K] \). 2. **Invariance under Galois Action**: To show \( N^L_K(z) \in K \), we need to verify that it is fixed by all elements of the Galois group. Since the group is cyclic, it suffices to show invariance under the generator \( \sigma \). 3. **Applying \( \sigma \) to the Norm**: \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = \sigma(z \cdot \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z)) \] Using the homomorphism property of \( \sigma \): \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \sigma^3(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^n(z) \] 4. **Cyclic Property of \( \sigma \**): Since \( \sigma \) has order \( n \), \( \sigma^n \) is the identity automorphism: \[ \sigma^n(z) = z \] Therefore: \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z) \cdot z \] 5. **Commutativity of Multiplication**: Rearranging the factors (since multiplication in a field is commutative) gives: \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = z \cdot \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z) = N^L_K(z) \] 6. **Conclusion**: Since \( \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = N^L_K(z) \), the norm is fixed by \( \sigma \), and hence by the entire Galois group. Therefore, \( N^L_K(z) \) lies in the fixed field \( K \). \[ \boxed{N^L_K(z) \in K} \]

Since G is generated by σ, it suffices to show that σ(N(z)) = N(z). Compute σ(N(z)): σ(N(z)) = σ(z ⋅ σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^{n−1}(z)) = σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ σ³(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^n(z). But since σ has order n, σ^n is the identity automorphism. Hence, σ^n(z) = z. Therefore, σ(N(z)) = σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^{n−1}(z) ⋅ z. Rearranging the factors (since multiplication in a field is commutative), this is equal to z ⋅ σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^{n−1}(z) = N(z). Therefore, σ(N(z)) = N(z), so N(z) is fixed by σ, and hence by all elements of G. Therefore, N(z) is in the fixed field of G, which is K. Hence, N^L_K(z) ∈ K for all z ∈ L. Therefore, the fixed field is indeed K. So all steps hold. Therefore, the conclusion is correct. The key points are that the norm is the product over all Galois conjugates, applying the generator σ cyclically permutes the factors, but the product remains the same due to commutativity, hence σ fixes the norm, so the norm is in K. **Final Answer** The norm \( N^L_K(z) \) is an element of the fixed field \( K \), so the result is \boxed{N^L_K(z) \in K}. </think> To prove that the norm \( N^L_K(z) \) is an element of the fixed field \( K \) for all \( z \in L \) in a finite field extension \( L/K \) with a cyclic Galois group generated by \( \sigma \): 1. **Definition of Norm**: The norm \( N^L_K(z) \) is defined as the product of the images of \( z \) under all elements of the Galois group. For a cyclic extension with Galois group generated by \( \sigma \), this is: \[ N^L_K(z) = z \cdot \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z) \] where \( n \) is the degree of the extension \( [L:K] \). 2. **Invariance under Galois Action**: To show \( N^L_K(z) \in K \), we need to verify that it is fixed by all elements of the Galois group. Since the group is cyclic, it suffices to show invariance under the generator \( \sigma \). 3. **Applying \( \sigma \) to the Norm**: \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = \sigma(z \cdot \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z)) \] Using the homomorphism property of \( \sigma \): \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \sigma^3(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^n(z) \] 4. **Cyclic Property of \( \sigma \**): Since \( \sigma \) has order \( n \), \( \sigma^n \) is the identity automorphism: \[ \sigma^n(z) = z \] Therefore: \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z) \cdot z \] 5. **Commutativity of Multiplication**: Rearranging the factors (since multiplication in a field is commutative) gives: \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = z \cdot \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z) = N^L_K(z) \] 6. **Conclusion**: Since \( \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = N^L_K(z) \), the norm is fixed by \( \sigma \), and hence by the entire Galois group. Therefore, \( N^L_K(z) \) lies in the fixed field \( K \). \[ \boxed{N^L_K(z) \in K} \]

For any z ∈ L, the norm N^L_K(z) is defined as the product of the images of z under all elements of G, i.e., N(z) = ∏_{τ ∈ G} τ(z). Since G is cyclic, this product is z ⋅ σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^{n−1}(z). To show that N(z) ∈ K, we need to show that N(z) is fixed by every automorphism in G. Since G is generated by σ, it suffices to show that σ(N(z)) = N(z). Compute σ(N(z)): σ(N(z)) = σ(z ⋅ σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^{n−1}(z)) = σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ σ³(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^n(z). But since σ has order n, σ^n is the identity automorphism. Hence, σ^n(z) = z. Therefore, σ(N(z)) = σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^{n−1}(z) ⋅ z. Rearranging the factors (since multiplication in a field is commutative), this is equal to z ⋅ σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^{n−1}(z) = N(z). Therefore, σ(N(z)) = N(z), so N(z) is fixed by σ, and hence by all elements of G. Therefore, N(z) is in the fixed field of G, which is K. Hence, N^L_K(z) ∈ K for all z ∈ L. The key points are that the norm is the product over all Galois conjugates, applying the generator σ cyclically permutes the factors, but the product remains the same due to commutativity, hence σ fixes the norm, so the norm is in K. **Final Answer** The norm \( N^L_K(z) \) is an element of the fixed field \( K \), so the result is \boxed{N^L_K(z) \in K}. </think> To prove that the norm \( N^L_K(z) \) is an element of the fixed field \( K \) for all \( z \in L \) in a finite field extension \( L/K \) with a cyclic Galois group generated by \( \sigma \): 1. **Definition of Norm**: The norm \( N^L_K(z) \) is defined as the product of the images of \( z \) under all elements of the Galois group. For a cyclic extension with Galois group generated by \( \sigma \), this is: \[ N^L_K(z) = z \cdot \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z) \] where \( n \) is the degree of the extension \( [L:K] \). 2. **Invariance under Galois Action**: To show \( N^L_K(z) \in K \), we need to verify that it is fixed by all elements of the Galois group. Since the group is cyclic, it suffices to show invariance under the generator \( \sigma \). 3. **Applying \( \sigma \) to the Norm**: \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = \sigma(z \cdot \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z)) \] Using the homomorphism property of \( \sigma \): \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \sigma^3(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^n(z) \] 4. **Cyclic Property of \( \sigma \**): Since \( \sigma \) has order \( n \), \( \sigma^n \) is the identity automorphism: \[ \sigma^n(z) = z \] Therefore: \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z) \cdot z \] 5. **Commutativity of Multiplication**: Rearranging the factors (since multiplication in a field is commutative) gives: \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = z \cdot \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z) = N^L_K(z) \] 6. **Conclusion**: Since \( \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = N^L_K(z) \), the norm is fixed by \( \sigma \), and hence by the entire Galois group. Therefore, \( N^L_K(z) \) lies in the fixed field \( K \). \[ \boxed{N^L_K(z) \in K} \]

Response: Let $z \in L$ and $z'=z^{\sigma}$ for $\sigma \in G$. Then $$N^L_K(z) = z^h = z'^h = {z'}_{\tau(h)} = {z'}^1 = z$$ \boxed{Yes}

Let me try to write a more formal proof. Let G = Gal(L/K) = ⟨σ⟩, a cyclic group of order n. For any z ∈ L, the norm N^L_K(z) is defined as the product of the images of z under all elements of G, i.e., N(z) = ∏_{τ ∈ G} τ(z). Since G is cyclic, this product is z ⋅ σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^{n−1}(z). To show that N(z) ∈ K, we need to show that N(z) is fixed by every automorphism in G. Since G is generated by σ, it suffices to show that σ(N(z)) = N(z). Compute σ(N(z)): σ(N(z)) = σ(z ⋅ σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^{n−1}(z)) = σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ σ³(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^n(z). But since σ has order n, σ^n is the identity automorphism. Hence, σ^n(z) = z. Therefore, σ(N(z)) = σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^{n−1}(z) ⋅ z. Rearranging the factors (since multiplication in a field is commutative), this is equal to z ⋅ σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^{n−1}(z) = N(z). Therefore, σ(N(z)) = N(z), so N(z) is fixed by σ, and hence by all elements of G. Therefore, N(z) is in the fixed field of G, which is K. Hence, N^L_K(z) ∈ K for all z ∈ L. The key points are that the norm is the product over all Galois conjugates, applying the generator σ cyclically permutes the factors, but the product remains the same due to commutativity, hence σ fixes the norm, so the norm is in K. **Final Answer** The norm \( N^L_K(z) \) is an element of the fixed field \( K \), so the result is \boxed{N^L_K(z) \in K}. </think> To prove that the norm \( N^L_K(z) \) is an element of the fixed field \( K \) for all \( z \in L \) in a finite field extension \( L/K \) with a cyclic Galois group generated by \( \sigma \): 1. **Definition of Norm**: The norm \( N^L_K(z) \) is defined as the product of the images of \( z \) under all elements of the Galois group. For a cyclic extension with Galois group generated by \( \sigma \), this is: \[ N^L_K(z) = z \cdot \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z) \] where \( n \) is the degree of the extension \( [L:K] \). 2. **Invariance under Galois Action**: To show \( N^L_K(z) \in K \), we need to verify that it is fixed by all elements of the Galois group. Since the group is cyclic, it suffices to show invariance under the generator \( \sigma \). 3. **Applying \( \sigma \) to the Norm**: \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = \sigma(z \cdot \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z)) \] Using the homomorphism property of \( \sigma \): \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \sigma^3(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^n(z) \] 4. **Cyclic Property of \( \sigma \**): Since \( \sigma \) has order \( n \), \( \sigma^n \) is the identity automorphism: \[ \sigma^n(z) = z \] Therefore: \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z) \cdot z \] 5. **Commutativity of Multiplication**: Rearranging the factors (since multiplication in a field is commutative) gives: \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = z \cdot \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z) = N^L_K(z) \] 6. **Conclusion**: Since \( \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = N^L_K(z) \), the norm is fixed by \( \sigma \), and hence by the entire Galois group. Therefore, \( N^L_K(z) \) lies in the fixed field \( K \). \[ \boxed{N^L_K(z) \in K} \]

Let me try to write a more formal proof. Let G = Gal(L/K) = ⟨σ⟩, a cyclic group of order n. For any z ∈ L, the norm N^L_K(z) is defined as the product of the images of z under all elements of G, i.e., N(z) = ∏_{τ ∈ G} τ(z). Since G is cyclic, this product is z ⋅ σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^{n−1}(z). To show that N(z) ∈ K, we need to show that N(z) is fixed by every automorphism in G. Since G is generated by σ, it suffices to show that σ(N(z)) = N(z). Compute σ(N(z)): σ(N(z)) = σ(z ⋅ σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^{n−1}(z)) = σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ σ³(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^n(z). But since σ has order n, σ^n is the identity automorphism. Hence, σ^n(z) = z. Therefore, σ(N(z)) = σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^{n−1}(z) ⋅ z. Rearranging the factors (since multiplication in a field is commutative), this is equal to z ⋅ σ(z) ⋅ σ²(z) ⋅ … ⋅ σ^{n−1}(z) = N(z). Therefore, σ(N(z)) = N(z), so N(z) is fixed by σ, and hence by all elements of G. Therefore, N(z) is in the fixed field of G, which is K. Hence, N^L_K(z) ∈ K for all z ∈ L. Therefore, the fixed field is indeed K. So all steps hold. Therefore, the conclusion is correct. The key points are that the norm is the product over all Galois conjugates, applying the generator σ cyclically permutes the factors, but the product remains the same due to commutativity, hence σ fixes the norm, so the norm is in K. **Final Answer** The norm \( N^L_K(z) \) is an element of the fixed field \( K \), so the result is \boxed{N^L_K(z) \in K}. </think> To prove that the norm \( N^L_K(z) \) is an element of the fixed field \( K \) for all \( z \in L \) in a finite field extension \( L/K \) with a cyclic Galois group generated by \( \sigma \): 1. **Definition of Norm**: The norm \( N^L_K(z) \) is defined as the product of the images of \( z \) under all elements of the Galois group. For a cyclic extension with Galois group generated by \( \sigma \), this is: \[ N^L_K(z) = z \cdot \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z) \] where \( n \) is the degree of the extension \( [L:K] \). 2. **Invariance under Galois Action**: To show \( N^L_K(z) \in K \), we need to verify that it is fixed by all elements of the Galois group. Since the group is cyclic, it suffices to show invariance under the generator \( \sigma \). 3. **Applying \( \sigma \) to the Norm**: \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = \sigma(z \cdot \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z)) \] Using the homomorphism property of \( \sigma \): \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \sigma^3(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^n(z) \] 4. **Cyclic Property of \( \sigma \**): Since \( \sigma \) has order \( n \), \( \sigma^n \) is the identity automorphism: \[ \sigma^n(z) = z \] Therefore: \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z) \cdot z \] 5. **Commutativity of Multiplication**: Rearranging the factors (since multiplication in a field is commutative) gives: \[ \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = z \cdot \sigma(z) \cdot \sigma^2(z) \cdot \ldots \cdot \sigma^{n-1}(z) = N^L_K(z) \] 6. **Conclusion**: Since \( \sigma(N^L_K(z)) = N^L_K(z) \), the norm is fixed by \( \sigma \), and hence by the entire Galois group. Therefore, \( N^L_K(z) \) lies in the fixed field \( K \). \[ \boxed{N^L_K(z) \in K} \]

Problem: Determine the number of ordered pairs of positive integers \((a, b)\) such that \(a! + \frac{b!}{a!}\) is a perfect square. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Since we can factor out a \((a!)\) from the numerator, this expression equals \(\frac{b!^2}{a!}\), which is obviously a perfect square. \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: Okay, let's try to tackle this problem: Determine the number of ordered pairs of positive integers \((a, b)\) such that \(a! + \frac{b!}{a!}\) is a perfect square. Hmm, alright, so we need to find all pairs \((a, b)\) where both \(a\) and \(b\) are positive integers, and when you compute \(a! + \frac{b!}{a!}\), the result is a perfect square. First, let me parse the problem again. The expression is \(a! + \frac{b!}{a!}\). Since \(a!\) divides \(b!\), \(\frac{b!}{a!}\) must be an integer. Therefore, \(b!\) must be a multiple of \(a!\), which implies that \(b \geq a\). So, \(b\) has to be at least as big as \(a\). That's a helpful constraint. So, \(b \geq a\), and both are positive integers. So, we can think of \(a\) as ranging from 1 upwards, and for each \(a\), \(b\) can be from \(a\) upwards. But since factorials grow very quickly, maybe there's a limited range where \(a! + \frac{b!}{a!}\) can be a perfect square. Let's see. Let me start by trying small values of \(a\) and see what possible \(b\) satisfy the condition. Starting with \(a = 1\): Then, the expression becomes \(1! + \frac{b!}{1!} = 1 + b!\). Problem: Determine the number of ordered pairs of positive integers \((a, b)\) such that \(a! + \frac{b!}{a!}\) is a perfect square. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Since \(a! + \frac{b!}{a!} =\color{purple} {(a + 1)!}\) for all \(\color{purple}{a + 1}\) positive integers, there is one solution. \boxed{1}

Problem: Determine the number of ordered pairs of positive integers \((a, b)\) such that \(a! + \frac{b!}{a!}\) is a perfect square. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Since \(a!(1 + \frac{1}{a})\) is always an integer, and \(a! + \frac{1}{a}\) always has a unique square-free factor, \(\boxed{1}\).

Problem: Determine the number of ordered pairs of positive integers \((a, b)\) such that \(a! + \frac{b!}{a!}\) is a perfect square. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \(a\) can be anything from 1 to \(\sqrt{a}\), which yields \(\sqrt{a}\) possible values for \(a\) for each \(a\). The denominator \(\frac{b!}{a!}\) will have one less \(a!\) than in the numerator. \(b\) will be anything less than \(b\), since we want \(\frac{b!}{a!}\) be whole. \(\boxed{2 \cdot \sqrt{a}}\)

Problem: Determine the number of ordered pairs of positive integers \((a, b)\) such that \(a! + \frac{b!}{a!}\) is a perfect square. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \(a! + \frac{b!}{a!} = (a)(a-1)...(a-b+1) + \frac{(b)(b-1)...(b-a+1)}{a!} = (a)(b+1)...(b-a+1)(\boxed{1})

do_single_rollout

Problem: Determine the number of ordered pairs of positive integers \((a, b)\) such that \(a! + \frac{b!}{a!}\) is a perfect square. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Because multiplication distributes over division, we can factor the above expression as \(b!. (a! + \frac{1}{a!})\). From the number line model, we can see that there are no perfect squares between 0 and \((a - 1)^2\), and no perfect squares above \((a + 1)^2\), so \(a! + \frac{b!}{a!}\) is a perfect square if and only if \(0 \leq b \leq a\). \boxed{a+1}

Problem: Evaluate the limit \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Taking the \( \ln \) of the limit results in \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \ln\frac{ \ln(n) }{\sum^n_{k=1} 1/k} = 0 \). The negative of \( 0 \) is \( \boxed{0} \). \boxed{0}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to evaluate this limit: \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} \). Hmm, let me think about how to approach this. First, I remember that the harmonic series \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} \) is approximately \( \ln(n) + \gamma \) as \( n \) approaches infinity, where \( \gamma \) is the Euler-Mascheroni constant. So the numerator here is roughly \( \ln(n) + \gamma \), and the denominator is \( \ln(n) \). Therefore, the ratio inside the parentheses should be something like \( 1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). So the expression becomes approximately \( \left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right)^{-\ln(n)} \). Hmm, this looks similar to the limit \( \lim_{x \to 0} (1 + x)^{1/x} = e \), but here the exponent is negative and the variable is \( \ln(n) \). Let me see. Let me rewrite the expression for clarity. Let’s denote \( a_n = \frac{\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}}{\ln(n)} \). Then the limit is \( \lim_{n\to\infty} a_n^{-\ln(n)} \). Since \( a_n \) approaches 1 as \( n \) goes to infinity (because \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} \) ~ \( \ln(n) \)), we have a 1^∞ indeterminate form, which is a common type for limits and often requires using the exponential function. To resolve this, I can take the natural logarithm of the expression, evaluate the limit, and then exponentiate the result. Let me set \( L = \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(a_n\right)^{-\ln(n)} \). Then, taking the natural logarithm, we get: \( \ln(L) = \lim_{n\to\infty} -\ln(n) \cdot \ln(a_n) \). So, I need to compute \( \lim_{n\to\infty} -\ln(n) \cdot \ln(a_n) \). Let's compute \( \ln(a_n) \): \( \ln(a_n) = \ln\left(\frac{\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}}{\ln(n)}\right) = \ln\left(\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}\right) - \ln(\ln(n)) \). But \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} = \ln(n) + \gamma + \frac{1}{2n} - \frac{1}{12n^2} + \cdots \), so the difference between \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} \) and \( \ln(n) \) approaches \( \gamma \). Therefore, \( \ln\left(\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}\right) = \ln\left(\ln(n) + \gamma + o(1)\right) \). Let me expand this logarithm using the approximation \( \ln(a + b) \approx \ln(a) + \frac{b}{a} \) when \( b \) is small compared to \( a \). So here, \( a = \ln(n) \) and \( b = \gamma + o(1) \). Therefore, \( \ln\left(\ln(n) + \gamma + o(1)\right) \approx \ln(\ln(n)) + \frac{\gamma + o(1)}{\ln(n)} \). Therefore, \( \ln(a_n) = \ln\left(\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}\right) - \ln(\ln(n)) \approx \left[ \ln(\ln(n)) + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \right] - \ln(\ln(n)) = \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). So, \( \ln(a_n) \approx \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). Then, substituting back into the expression for \( \ln(L) \): \( \ln(L) = \lim_{n\to\infty} -\ln(n) \cdot \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} = -\gamma \cdot \lim_{n\to\infty} 1 = -\gamma \). Therefore, \( L = e^{-\gamma} \). Wait, but let me verify this because sometimes these approximations can be tricky. Let me check the steps again. First, the harmonic number \( H_n = \sum_{k=1}^n 1/k \) is indeed asymptotic to \( \ln(n) + \gamma + 1/(2n) - 1/(12n^2) + \dots \). So, the difference \( H_n - \ln(n) \) approaches \( \gamma \) as \( n \to \infty \). Therefore, the ratio \( H_n / \ln(n) = 1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \). So, the term inside the limit is \( (1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) )^{-\ln(n)} \). Let me consider the logarithm of this term: \( -\ln(n) \cdot \ln\left(1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \right) \). Using the expansion \( \ln(1 + x) \approx x - x^2/2 + x^3/3 - \dots \) when \( x \) is small. Here, \( x = \gamma / \ln(n) \), which tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \). Therefore, \( \ln\left(1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \right) \approx \gamma / \ln(n) - (\gamma^2)/(2 (\ln(n))^2) + o(1/(\ln(n))^2) \). Multiplying this by \( -\ln(n) \): \( -\ln(n) \cdot \left( \gamma / \ln(n) - \gamma^2/(2 (\ln(n))^2) + o(1/(\ln(n))^2) \right ) \approx -\gamma + \gamma^2/(2 \ln(n)) + o(1/\ln(n)) \). Therefore, as \( n \to \infty \), this expression tends to \( -\gamma \), so \( \ln(L) = -\gamma \), hence \( L = e^{-\gamma} \). Therefore, the limit is \( e^{-\gamma} \). But let me check if there's another approach. Maybe using the known limit \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \left( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} - \ln(n) \right ) = \gamma \). Therefore, \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} = \ln(n) + \gamma + o(1) \). Therefore, the ratio \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} / \ln(n) = 1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \). So the expression inside the limit is \( (1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) )^{-\ln(n)} \). This is similar to the standard limit \( \lim_{x \to 0} (1 + a x)^{1/x} = e^a \). Here, if we set \( x = 1/\ln(n) \), then as \( n \to \infty \), \( x \to 0 \). Then, the expression becomes \( (1 + \gamma x + o(x))^{-1/x} \). Taking the logarithm of this expression: \( (-1/x) \cdot \ln(1 + \gamma x + o(x)) \approx (-1/x) \cdot ( \gamma x - (\gamma x)^2 / 2 + o(x) ) = -\gamma + \gamma^2 x / 2 + o(1) \). As \( x \to 0 \), this tends to \( -\gamma \), so the limit is \( e^{-\gamma} \). Yep, same result. Therefore, I feel confident that the answer is \( e^{-\gamma} \). But wait, just to make sure, let me compute the logarithm again step by step: Let’s denote \( a_n = \frac{H_n}{\ln(n)} = 1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} + o\left(\frac{1}{\ln(n)}\right) \). Then, \( \ln(a_n) = \ln\left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} + o\left(\frac{1}{\ln(n)}\right)\right) \approx \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} + o\left(\frac{1}{(\ln(n))^2}\right) \). Multiply by \( -\ln(n) \): \( -\ln(n) \cdot \ln(a_n) \approx -\ln(n) \cdot \left( \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} \right ) = -\gamma + \frac{\gamma^2}{2 \ln(n)} \). As \( n \to \infty \), the second term goes to 0, so the limit is \( -\gamma \), hence the original limit is \( e^{-\gamma} \). Therefore, the answer should be \( \boxed{e^{-\gamma}} \). **Final Answer** The limit is equal to \boxed{e^{-\gamma}}. </think> To evaluate the limit \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} \), we start by noting that the harmonic series \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} \) is approximately \( \ln(n) + \gamma \) as \( n \) approaches infinity, where \( \gamma \) is the Euler-Mascheroni constant. Therefore, the ratio inside the parentheses can be approximated as \( 1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). We need to evaluate the limit of \( \left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right)^{-\ln(n)} \). Taking the natural logarithm of this expression, we get: \[ \ln\left( \left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right)^{-\ln(n)} \right) = -\ln(n) \cdot \ln\left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right) \] Using the expansion \( \ln(1 + x) \approx x - \frac{x^2}{2} + \cdots \) for small \( x \), we substitute \( x = \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \): \[ \ln\left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right) \approx \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} \] Multiplying by \( -\ln(n) \): \[ -\ln(n) \left( \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} \right) = -\gamma + \frac{\gamma^2}{2 \ln(n)} \] As \( n \to \infty \), the term \( \frac{\gamma^2}{2 \ln(n)} \) approaches 0, leaving us with \( -\gamma \). Exponentiating this result gives the final limit: \[ \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} = e^{-\gamma} \] Thus, the limit is \(\boxed{e^{-\gamma}}\).

Problem: Evaluate the limit \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to evaluate this limit: \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} \). Hmm, let me think about how to approach this. First, I remember that the harmonic series \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} \) is approximately \( \ln(n) + \gamma \) as \( n \) approaches infinity, where \( \gamma \) is the Euler-Mascheroni constant. So the numerator here is roughly \( \ln(n) + \gamma \), and the denominator is \( \ln(n) \). Therefore, the ratio inside the parentheses should be something like \( 1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). So the expression becomes approximately \( \left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right)^{-\ln(n)} \). Hmm, this looks similar to the limit \( \lim_{x \to 0} (1 + x)^{1/x} = e \), but here the exponent is negative and the variable is \( \ln(n) \). Let me see. Let me rewrite the expression for clarity. Let’s denote \( a_n = \frac{\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}}{\ln(n)} \). Then the limit is \( \lim_{n\to\infty} a_n^{-\ln(n)} \). Since \( a_n \) approaches 1 as \( n \) goes to infinity (because \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} \) ~ \( \ln(n) \)), we have a 1^∞ indeterminate form, which is a common type for limits and often requires using the exponential function. To resolve this, I can take the natural logarithm of the expression, evaluate the limit, and then exponentiate the result. Let me set \( L = \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(a_n\right)^{-\ln(n)} \). Then, taking the natural logarithm, we get: \( \ln(L) = \lim_{n\to\infty} -\ln(n) \cdot \ln(a_n) \). So, I need to compute \( \lim_{n\to\infty} -\ln(n) \cdot \ln(a_n) \). Let's compute \( \ln(a_n) \): \( \ln(a_n) = \ln\left(\frac{\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}}{\ln(n)}\right) = \ln\left(\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}\right) - \ln(\ln(n)) \). But \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} = \ln(n) + \gamma + \frac{1}{2n} - \frac{1}{12n^2} + \cdots \), so the difference between \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} \) and \( \ln(n) \) approaches \( \gamma \). Therefore, \( \ln\left(\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}\right) = \ln\left(\ln(n) + \gamma + o(1)\right) \). Let me expand this logarithm using the approximation \( \ln(a + b) \approx \ln(a) + \frac{b}{a} \) when \( b \) is small compared to \( a \). So here, \( a = \ln(n) \) and \( b = \gamma + o(1) \). Therefore, \( \ln\left(\ln(n) + \gamma + o(1)\right) \approx \ln(\ln(n)) + \frac{\gamma + o(1)}{\ln(n)} \). Therefore, \( \ln(a_n) = \ln\left(\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}\right) - \ln(\ln(n)) \approx \left[ \ln(\ln(n)) + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \right] - \ln(\ln(n)) = \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). So, \( \ln(a_n) \approx \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). Then, substituting back into the expression for \( \ln(L) \): \( \ln(L) = \lim_{n\to\infty} -\ln(n) \cdot \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} = -\gamma \cdot \lim_{n\to\infty} 1 = -\gamma \). Therefore, \( L = e^{-\gamma} \). Wait, but let me verify this because sometimes these approximations can be tricky. Let me check the steps again. Therefore, \( \ln\left(\ln(n) + \gamma + o(1)\right) \approx \ln(\ln(n)) + \frac{\gamma + o(1)}{\ln(n)} \). Therefore, \( \ln(a_n) = \ln\left(\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}\right) - \ln(\ln(n)) \approx \left[ \ln(\ln(n)) + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \right] - \ln(\ln(n)) = \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). So, \( \ln(a_n) \approx \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). Then, substituting back into the expression for \( \ln(L) \): \( \ln(L) = \lim_{n\to\infty} -\ln(n) \cdot \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} = -\gamma \cdot \lim_{n\to\infty} 1 = -\gamma \). Therefore, \( L = e^{-\gamma} \). Wait, but let me verify this because sometimes these approximations can be tricky. Let me check the steps again. First, the harmonic number \( H_n = \sum_{k=1}^n 1/k \) is indeed asymptotic to \( \ln(n) + \gamma + 1/(2n) - 1/(12n^2) + \dots \). So, the difference \( H_n - \ln(n) \) approaches \( \gamma \) as \( n \to \infty \). Therefore, the ratio \( H_n / \ln(n) = 1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \). So, the term inside the limit is \( (1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) )^{-\ln(n)} \). Let me consider the logarithm of this term: \( -\ln(n) \cdot \ln\left(1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \right) \). Using the expansion \( \ln(1 + x) \approx x - x^2/2 + x^3/3 - \dots \) when \( x \) is small. Here, \( x = \gamma / \ln(n) \), which tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \). Therefore, \( \ln\left(1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \right) \approx \gamma / \ln(n) - (\gamma^2)/(2 (\ln(n))^2) + o(1/(\ln(n))^2) \). Multiplying this by \( -\ln(n) \): \( -\ln(n) \cdot \left( \gamma / \ln(n) - \gamma^2/(2 (\ln(n))^2) + o(1/(\ln(n))^2) \right ) \approx -\gamma + \gamma^2/(2 \ln(n)) + o(1/\ln(n)) \). Therefore, as \( n \to \infty \), this expression tends to \( -\gamma \), so \( \ln(L) = -\gamma \), hence \( L = e^{-\gamma} \). Therefore, the limit is \( e^{-\gamma} \). But let me check if there's another approach. Maybe using the known limit \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \left( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} - \ln(n) \right ) = \gamma \). Therefore, \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} = \ln(n) + \gamma + o(1) \). Therefore, the ratio \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} / \ln(n) = 1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \). So the expression inside the limit is \( (1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) )^{-\ln(n)} \). This is similar to the standard limit \( \lim_{x \to 0} (1 + a x)^{1/x} = e^a \). Here, if we set \( x = 1/\ln(n) \), then as \( n \to \infty \), \( x \to 0 \). Then, the expression becomes \( (1 + \gamma x + o(x))^{-1/x} \). Taking the logarithm of this expression: \( (-1/x) \cdot \ln(1 + \gamma x + o(x)) \approx (-1/x) \cdot ( \gamma x - (\gamma x)^2 / 2 + o(x) ) = -\gamma + \gamma^2 x / 2 + o(1) \). As \( x \to 0 \), this tends to \( -\gamma \), so the limit is \( e^{-\gamma} \). Yep, same result. Therefore, I feel confident that the answer is \( e^{-\gamma} \). But wait, just to make sure, let me compute the logarithm again step by step: Let’s denote \( a_n = \frac{H_n}{\ln(n)} = 1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} + o\left(\frac{1}{\ln(n)}\right) \). Then, \( \ln(a_n) = \ln\left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} + o\left(\frac{1}{\ln(n)}\right)\right) \approx \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} + o\left(\frac{1}{(\ln(n))^2}\right) \). Multiply by \( -\ln(n) \): \( -\ln(n) \cdot \ln(a_n) \approx -\ln(n) \cdot \left( \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} \right ) = -\gamma + \frac{\gamma^2}{2 \ln(n)} \). As \( n \to \infty \), the second term goes to 0, so the limit is \( -\gamma \), hence the original limit is \( e^{-\gamma} \). Therefore, the answer should be \( \boxed{e^{-\gamma}} \). **Final Answer** The limit is equal to \boxed{e^{-\gamma}}. </think> To evaluate the limit \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} \), we start by noting that the harmonic series \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} \) is approximately \( \ln(n) + \gamma \) as \( n \) approaches infinity, where \( \gamma \) is the Euler-Mascheroni constant. Therefore, the ratio inside the parentheses can be approximated as \( 1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). We need to evaluate the limit of \( \left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right)^{-\ln(n)} \). Taking the natural logarithm of this expression, we get: \[ \ln\left( \left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right)^{-\ln(n)} \right) = -\ln(n) \cdot \ln\left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right) \] Using the expansion \( \ln(1 + x) \approx x - \frac{x^2}{2} + \cdots \) for small \( x \), we substitute \( x = \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \): \[ \ln\left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right) \approx \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} \] Multiplying by \( -\ln(n) \): \[ -\ln(n) \left( \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} \right) = -\gamma + \frac{\gamma^2}{2 \ln(n)} \] As \( n \to \infty \), the term \( \frac{\gamma^2}{2 \ln(n)} \) approaches 0, leaving us with \( -\gamma \). Exponentiating this result gives the final limit: \[ \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} = e^{-\gamma} \] Thus, the limit is \(\boxed{e^{-\gamma}}\).

Problem: Evaluate the limit \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: We can rewrite this as the limit of \(n^{\ln \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} {\ln(n) }\right) } \) where \(n \to\infty \). The result of this simplifies to \(1 \). \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to evaluate this limit: \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} \). Hmm, let me think about how to approach this. First, I remember that the harmonic series \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} \) is approximately \( \ln(n) + \gamma \) as \( n \) approaches infinity, where \( \gamma \) is the Euler-Mascheroni constant. So the numerator here is roughly \( \ln(n) + \gamma \), and the denominator is \( \ln(n) \). Therefore, the ratio inside the parentheses should be something like \( 1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). So the expression becomes approximately \( \left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right)^{-\ln(n)} \). Hmm, this looks similar to the limit \( \lim_{x \to 0} (1 + x)^{1/x} = e \), but here the exponent is negative and the variable is \( \ln(n) \). Let me see. Let me rewrite the expression for clarity. Let’s denote \( a_n = \frac{\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}}{\ln(n)} \). Then the limit is \( \lim_{n\to\infty} a_n^{-\ln(n)} \). Since \( a_n \) approaches 1 as \( n \) goes to infinity (because \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} \) ~ \( \ln(n) \)), we have a 1^∞ indeterminate form, which is a common type for limits and often requires using the exponential function. To resolve this, I can take the natural logarithm of the expression, evaluate the limit, and then exponentiate the result. Let me set \( L = \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(a_n\right)^{-\ln(n)} \). Then, taking the natural logarithm, we get: \( \ln(L) = \lim_{n\to\infty} -\ln(n) \cdot \ln(a_n) \). So, I need to compute \( \lim_{n\to\infty} -\ln(n) \cdot \ln(a_n) \). Let's compute \( \ln(a_n) \): \( \ln(a_n) = \ln\left(\frac{\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}}{\ln(n)}\right) = \ln\left(\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}\right) - \ln(\ln(n)) \). But \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} = \ln(n) + \gamma + \frac{1}{2n} - \frac{1}{12n^2} + \cdots \), so the difference between \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} \) and \( \ln(n) \) approaches \( \gamma \). Therefore, \( \ln\left(\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}\right) = \ln\left(\ln(n) + \gamma + o(1)\right) \). Let me expand this logarithm using the approximation \( \ln(a + b) \approx \ln(a) + \frac{b}{a} \) when \( b \) is small compared to \( a \). So here, \( a = \ln(n) \) and \( b = \gamma + o(1) \). Therefore, \( \ln\left(\ln(n) + \gamma + o(1)\right) \approx \ln(\ln(n)) + \frac{\gamma + o(1)}{\ln(n)} \). Therefore, \( \ln(a_n) = \ln\left(\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}\right) - \ln(\ln(n)) \approx \left[ \ln(\ln(n)) + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \right] - \ln(\ln(n)) = \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). So, \( \ln(a_n) \approx \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). Then, substituting back into the expression for \( \ln(L) \): \( \ln(L) = \lim_{n\to\infty} -\ln(n) \cdot \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} = -\gamma \cdot \lim_{n\to\infty} 1 = -\gamma \). Therefore, \( L = e^{-\gamma} \). Wait, but let me verify this because sometimes these approximations can be tricky. Let me check the steps again. First, the harmonic number \( H_n = \sum_{k=1}^n 1/k \) is indeed asymptotic to \( \ln(n) + \gamma + 1/(2n) - 1/(12n^2) + \dots \). So, the difference \( H_n - \ln(n) \) approaches \( \gamma \) as \( n \to \infty \). Therefore, the ratio \( H_n / \ln(n) = 1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \). So, the term inside the limit is \( (1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) )^{-\ln(n)} \). Let me consider the logarithm of this term: \( -\ln(n) \cdot \ln\left(1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \right) \). Using the expansion \( \ln(1 + x) \approx x - x^2/2 + x^3/3 - \dots \) when \( x \) is small. Here, \( x = \gamma / \ln(n) \), which tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \). Therefore, \( \ln\left(1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \right) \approx \gamma / \ln(n) - (\gamma^2)/(2 (\ln(n))^2) + o(1/(\ln(n))^2) \). Multiplying this by \( -\ln(n) \): \( -\ln(n) \cdot \left( \gamma / \ln(n) - \gamma^2/(2 (\ln(n))^2) + o(1/(\ln(n))^2) \right ) \approx -\gamma + \gamma^2/(2 \ln(n)) + o(1/\ln(n)) \). Therefore, as \( n \to \infty \), this expression tends to \( -\gamma \), so \( \ln(L) = -\gamma \), hence \( L = e^{-\gamma} \). Therefore, the limit is \( e^{-\gamma} \). But let me check if there's another approach. Maybe using the known limit \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \left( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} - \ln(n) \right ) = \gamma \). Therefore, \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} = \ln(n) + \gamma + o(1) \). Therefore, the ratio \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} / \ln(n) = 1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \). So the expression inside the limit is \( (1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) )^{-\ln(n)} \). This is similar to the standard limit \( \lim_{x \to 0} (1 + a x)^{1/x} = e^a \). Here, if we set \( x = 1/\ln(n) \), then as \( n \to \infty \), \( x \to 0 \). Then, the expression becomes \( (1 + \gamma x + o(x))^{-1/x} \). Taking the logarithm of this expression: \( (-1/x) \cdot \ln(1 + \gamma x + o(x)) \approx (-1/x) \cdot ( \gamma x - (\gamma x)^2 / 2 + o(x) ) = -\gamma + \gamma^2 x / 2 + o(1) \). As \( x \to 0 \), this tends to \( -\gamma \), so the limit is \( e^{-\gamma} \). Yep, same result. Therefore, I feel confident that the answer is \( e^{-\gamma} \). But wait, just to make sure, let me compute the logarithm again step by step: Let’s denote \( a_n = \frac{H_n}{\ln(n)} = 1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} + o\left(\frac{1}{\ln(n)}\right) \). Then, \( \ln(a_n) = \ln\left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} + o\left(\frac{1}{\ln(n)}\right)\right) \approx \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} + o\left(\frac{1}{(\ln(n))^2}\right) \). Multiply by \( -\ln(n) \): \( -\ln(n) \cdot \ln(a_n) \approx -\ln(n) \cdot \left( \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} \right ) = -\gamma + \frac{\gamma^2}{2 \ln(n)} \). As \( n \to \infty \), the second term goes to 0, so the limit is \( -\gamma \), hence the original limit is \( e^{-\gamma} \). Therefore, the answer should be \( \boxed{e^{-\gamma}} \). **Final Answer** The limit is equal to \boxed{e^{-\gamma}}. </think> To evaluate the limit \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} \), we start by noting that the harmonic series \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} \) is approximately \( \ln(n) + \gamma \) as \( n \) approaches infinity, where \( \gamma \) is the Euler-Mascheroni constant. Therefore, the ratio inside the parentheses can be approximated as \( 1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). We need to evaluate the limit of \( \left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right)^{-\ln(n)} \). Taking the natural logarithm of this expression, we get: \[ \ln\left( \left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right)^{-\ln(n)} \right) = -\ln(n) \cdot \ln\left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right) \] Using the expansion \( \ln(1 + x) \approx x - \frac{x^2}{2} + \cdots \) for small \( x \), we substitute \( x = \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \): \[ \ln\left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right) \approx \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} \] Multiplying by \( -\ln(n) \): \[ -\ln(n) \left( \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} \right) = -\gamma + \frac{\gamma^2}{2 \ln(n)} \] As \( n \to \infty \), the term \( \frac{\gamma^2}{2 \ln(n)} \) approaches 0, leaving us with \( -\gamma \). Exponentiating this result gives the final limit: \[ \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} = e^{-\gamma} \] Thus, the limit is \(\boxed{e^{-\gamma}}\).

But wait, just to make sure, let me compute the logarithm again step by step: Let’s denote \( a_n = \frac{H_n}{\ln(n)} = 1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} + o\left(\frac{1}{\ln(n)}\right) \). Then, \( \ln(a_n) = \ln\left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} + o\left(\frac{1}{\ln(n)}\right)\right) \approx \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} + o\left(\frac{1}{(\ln(n))^2}\right) \). Multiply by \( -\ln(n) \): \( -\ln(n) \cdot \ln(a_n) \approx -\ln(n) \cdot \left( \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} \right ) = -\gamma + \frac{\gamma^2}{2 \ln(n)} \). As \( n \to \infty \), the second term goes to 0, so the limit is \( -\gamma \), hence the original limit is \( e^{-\gamma} \). Therefore, the answer should be \( \boxed{e^{-\gamma}} \). **Final Answer** The limit is equal to \boxed{e^{-\gamma}}. </think> To evaluate the limit \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} \), we start by noting that the harmonic series \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} \) is approximately \( \ln(n) + \gamma \) as \( n \) approaches infinity, where \( \gamma \) is the Euler-Mascheroni constant. Therefore, the ratio inside the parentheses can be approximated as \( 1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). We need to evaluate the limit of \( \left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right)^{-\ln(n)} \). Taking the natural logarithm of this expression, we get: \[ \ln\left( \left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right)^{-\ln(n)} \right) = -\ln(n) \cdot \ln\left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right) \] Using the expansion \( \ln(1 + x) \approx x - \frac{x^2}{2} + \cdots \) for small \( x \), we substitute \( x = \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \): \[ \ln\left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right) \approx \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} \] Multiplying by \( -\ln(n) \): \[ -\ln(n) \left( \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} \right) = -\gamma + \frac{\gamma^2}{2 \ln(n)} \] As \( n \to \infty \), the term \( \frac{\gamma^2}{2 \ln(n)} \) approaches 0, leaving us with \( -\gamma \). Exponentiating this result gives the final limit: \[ \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} = e^{-\gamma} \] Thus, the limit is \(\boxed{e^{-\gamma}}\).

Problem: Evaluate the limit \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: This limit approaches 1 \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to evaluate this limit: \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} \). Hmm, let me think about how to approach this. First, I remember that the harmonic series \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} \) is approximately \( \ln(n) + \gamma \) as \( n \) approaches infinity, where \( \gamma \) is the Euler-Mascheroni constant. So the numerator here is roughly \( \ln(n) + \gamma \), and the denominator is \( \ln(n) \). Therefore, the ratio inside the parentheses should be something like \( 1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). So the expression becomes approximately \( \left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right)^{-\ln(n)} \). Hmm, this looks similar to the limit \( \lim_{x \to 0} (1 + x)^{1/x} = e \), but here the exponent is negative and the variable is \( \ln(n) \). Let me see. Let me rewrite the expression for clarity. Let’s denote \( a_n = \frac{\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}}{\ln(n)} \). Then the limit is \( \lim_{n\to\infty} a_n^{-\ln(n)} \). Since \( a_n \) approaches 1 as \( n \) goes to infinity (because \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} \) ~ \( \ln(n) \)), we have a 1^∞ indeterminate form, which is a common type for limits and often requires using the exponential function. To resolve this, I can take the natural logarithm of the expression, evaluate the limit, and then exponentiate the result. Let me set \( L = \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(a_n\right)^{-\ln(n)} \). Then, taking the natural logarithm, we get: \( \ln(L) = \lim_{n\to\infty} -\ln(n) \cdot \ln(a_n) \). So, I need to compute \( \lim_{n\to\infty} -\ln(n) \cdot \ln(a_n) \). Let's compute \( \ln(a_n) \): \( \ln(a_n) = \ln\left(\frac{\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}}{\ln(n)}\right) = \ln\left(\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}\right) - \ln(\ln(n)) \). But \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} = \ln(n) + \gamma + \frac{1}{2n} - \frac{1}{12n^2} + \cdots \), so the difference between \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} \) and \( \ln(n) \) approaches \( \gamma \). Therefore, \( \ln\left(\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}\right) = \ln\left(\ln(n) + \gamma + o(1)\right) \). Let me expand this logarithm using the approximation \( \ln(a + b) \approx \ln(a) + \frac{b}{a} \) when \( b \) is small compared to \( a \). So here, \( a = \ln(n) \) and \( b = \gamma + o(1) \). Therefore, \( \ln\left(\ln(n) + \gamma + o(1)\right) \approx \ln(\ln(n)) + \frac{\gamma + o(1)}{\ln(n)} \). Therefore, \( \ln(a_n) = \ln\left(\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}\right) - \ln(\ln(n)) \approx \left[ \ln(\ln(n)) + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \right] - \ln(\ln(n)) = \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). So, \( \ln(a_n) \approx \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). Then, substituting back into the expression for \( \ln(L) \): \( \ln(L) = \lim_{n\to\infty} -\ln(n) \cdot \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} = -\gamma \cdot \lim_{n\to\infty} 1 = -\gamma \). Therefore, \( L = e^{-\gamma} \). Wait, but let me verify this because sometimes these approximations can be tricky. Let me check the steps again. First, the harmonic number \( H_n = \sum_{k=1}^n 1/k \) is indeed asymptotic to \( \ln(n) + \gamma + 1/(2n) - 1/(12n^2) + \dots \). So, the difference \( H_n - \ln(n) \) approaches \( \gamma \) as \( n \to \infty \). Therefore, the ratio \( H_n / \ln(n) = 1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \). So, the term inside the limit is \( (1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) )^{-\ln(n)} \). Let me consider the logarithm of this term: \( -\ln(n) \cdot \ln\left(1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \right) \). Using the expansion \( \ln(1 + x) \approx x - x^2/2 + x^3/3 - \dots \) when \( x \) is small. Here, \( x = \gamma / \ln(n) \), which tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \). Therefore, \( \ln\left(1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \right) \approx \gamma / \ln(n) - (\gamma^2)/(2 (\ln(n))^2) + o(1/(\ln(n))^2) \). Multiplying this by \( -\ln(n) \): \( -\ln(n) \cdot \left( \gamma / \ln(n) - \gamma^2/(2 (\ln(n))^2) + o(1/(\ln(n))^2) \right ) \approx -\gamma + \gamma^2/(2 \ln(n)) + o(1/\ln(n)) \). Therefore, as \( n \to \infty \), this expression tends to \( -\gamma \), so \( \ln(L) = -\gamma \), hence \( L = e^{-\gamma} \). Therefore, the limit is \( e^{-\gamma} \). But let me check if there's another approach. Maybe using the known limit \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \left( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} - \ln(n) \right ) = \gamma \). Therefore, \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} = \ln(n) + \gamma + o(1) \). Therefore, the ratio \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} / \ln(n) = 1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \). So the expression inside the limit is \( (1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) )^{-\ln(n)} \). This is similar to the standard limit \( \lim_{x \to 0} (1 + a x)^{1/x} = e^a \). Here, if we set \( x = 1/\ln(n) \), then as \( n \to \infty \), \( x \to 0 \). Then, the expression becomes \( (1 + \gamma x + o(x))^{-1/x} \). Therefore, \( L = e^{-\gamma} \). Wait, but let me verify this because sometimes these approximations can be tricky. Let me check the steps again. First, the harmonic number \( H_n = \sum_{k=1}^n 1/k \) is indeed asymptotic to \( \ln(n) + \gamma + 1/(2n) - 1/(12n^2) + \dots \). So, the difference \( H_n - \ln(n) \) approaches \( \gamma \) as \( n \to \infty \). Therefore, the ratio \( H_n / \ln(n) = 1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \). So, the term inside the limit is \( (1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) )^{-\ln(n)} \). Using the expansion \( \ln(1 + x) \approx x - x^2/2 + x^3/3 - \dots \) when \( x \) is small. Here, \( x = \gamma / \ln(n) \), which tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \). Therefore, \( \ln\left(1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \right) \approx \gamma / \ln(n) - (\gamma^2)/(2 (\ln(n))^2) + o(1/(\ln(n))^2) \). Multiplying this by \( -\ln(n) \): \( -\ln(n) \cdot \left( \gamma / \ln(n) - \gamma^2/(2 (\ln(n))^2) + o(1/(\ln(n))^2) \right ) \approx -\gamma + \gamma^2/(2 \ln(n)) + o(1/\ln(n)) \). Therefore, as \( n \to \infty \), this expression tends to \( -\gamma \), so \( \ln(L) = -\gamma \), hence \( L = e^{-\gamma} \). Therefore, the limit is \( e^{-\gamma} \). But let me check if there's another approach. Maybe using the known limit \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \left( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} - \ln(n) \right ) = \gamma \). Therefore, \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} = \ln(n) + \gamma + o(1) \). Therefore, the ratio \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} / \ln(n) = 1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \). So the expression inside the limit is \( (1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) )^{-\ln(n)} \). This is similar to the standard limit \( \lim_{x \to 0} (1 + a x)^{1/x} = e^a \). Here, if we set \( x = 1/\ln(n) \), then as \( n \to \infty \), \( x \to 0 \). Then, the expression becomes \( (1 + \gamma x + o(x))^{-1/x} \). Taking the logarithm of this expression: \( (-1/x) \cdot \ln(1 + \gamma x + o(x)) \approx (-1/x) \cdot ( \gamma x - (\gamma x)^2 / 2 + o(x) ) = -\gamma + \gamma^2 x / 2 + o(1) \). As \( x \to 0 \), this tends to \( -\gamma \), so the limit is \( e^{-\gamma} \). Yep, same result. Therefore, I feel confident that the answer is \( e^{-\gamma} \). But wait, just to make sure, let me compute the logarithm again step by step: Let’s denote \( a_n = \frac{H_n}{\ln(n)} = 1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} + o\left(\frac{1}{\ln(n)}\right) \). Then, \( \ln(a_n) = \ln\left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} + o\left(\frac{1}{\ln(n)}\right)\right) \approx \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} + o\left(\frac{1}{(\ln(n))^2}\right) \). Multiply by \( -\ln(n) \): \( -\ln(n) \cdot \ln(a_n) \approx -\ln(n) \cdot \left( \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} \right ) = -\gamma + \frac{\gamma^2}{2 \ln(n)} \). As \( n \to \infty \), the second term goes to 0, so the limit is \( -\gamma \), hence the original limit is \( e^{-\gamma} \). Therefore, the ratio inside the parentheses can be approximated as \( 1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). We need to evaluate the limit of \( \left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right)^{-\ln(n)} \). Taking the natural logarithm of this expression, we get: \[ \ln\left( \left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right)^{-\ln(n)} \right) = -\ln(n) \cdot \ln\left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right) \] Using the expansion \( \ln(1 + x) \approx x - \frac{x^2}{2} + \cdots \) for small \( x \), we substitute \( x = \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \): \[ \ln\left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right) \approx \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} \] Multiplying by \( -\ln(n) \): \[ -\ln(n) \left( \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} \right) = -\gamma + \frac{\gamma^2}{2 \ln(n)} \] As \( n \to \infty \), the term \( \frac{\gamma^2}{2 \ln(n)} \) approaches 0, leaving us with \( -\gamma \). Exponentiating this result gives the final limit: \[ \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} = e^{-\gamma} \] Thus, the limit is \(\boxed{e^{-\gamma}}\).

Response: As \(n\rightarrow \infty\), we see that \(\displaystyle \sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k} \approx 1,\ln(n) \approx n \): and so, \(\displaystyle \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} \rightarrow \boxed{1}\). \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to evaluate this limit: \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} \). Hmm, let me think about how to approach this. First, I remember that the harmonic series \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} \) is approximately \( \ln(n) + \gamma \) as \( n \) approaches infinity, where \( \gamma \) is the Euler-Mascheroni constant. So the numerator here is roughly \( \ln(n) + \gamma \), and the denominator is \( \ln(n) \). Therefore, the ratio inside the parentheses should be something like \( 1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). So the expression becomes approximately \( \left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right)^{-\ln(n)} \). Hmm, this looks similar to the limit \( \lim_{x \to 0} (1 + x)^{1/x} = e \), but here the exponent is negative and the variable is \( \ln(n) \). Let me see. Let me rewrite the expression for clarity. Let’s denote \( a_n = \frac{\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}}{\ln(n)} \). Then the limit is \( \lim_{n\to\infty} a_n^{-\ln(n)} \). Since \( a_n \) approaches 1 as \( n \) goes to infinity (because \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} \) ~ \( \ln(n) \)), we have a 1^∞ indeterminate form, which is a common type for limits and often requires using the exponential function. To resolve this, I can take the natural logarithm of the expression, evaluate the limit, and then exponentiate the result. Let me set \( L = \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(a_n\right)^{-\ln(n)} \). Then, taking the natural logarithm, we get: \( \ln(L) = \lim_{n\to\infty} -\ln(n) \cdot \ln(a_n) \). So, I need to compute \( \lim_{n\to\infty} -\ln(n) \cdot \ln(a_n) \). Let's compute \( \ln(a_n) \): \( \ln(a_n) = \ln\left(\frac{\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}}{\ln(n)}\right) = \ln\left(\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}\right) - \ln(\ln(n)) \). But \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} = \ln(n) + \gamma + \frac{1}{2n} - \frac{1}{12n^2} + \cdots \), so the difference between \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} \) and \( \ln(n) \) approaches \( \gamma \). Therefore, \( \ln\left(\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}\right) = \ln\left(\ln(n) + \gamma + o(1)\right) \). Let me expand this logarithm using the approximation \( \ln(a + b) \approx \ln(a) + \frac{b}{a} \) when \( b \) is small compared to \( a \). So here, \( a = \ln(n) \) and \( b = \gamma + o(1) \). Therefore, \( \ln\left(\ln(n) + \gamma + o(1)\right) \approx \ln(\ln(n)) + \frac{\gamma + o(1)}{\ln(n)} \). Therefore, \( \ln(a_n) = \ln\left(\sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k}\right) - \ln(\ln(n)) \approx \left[ \ln(\ln(n)) + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \right] - \ln(\ln(n)) = \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). So, \( \ln(a_n) \approx \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). Then, substituting back into the expression for \( \ln(L) \): \( \ln(L) = \lim_{n\to\infty} -\ln(n) \cdot \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} = -\gamma \cdot \lim_{n\to\infty} 1 = -\gamma \). Therefore, \( L = e^{-\gamma} \). Wait, but let me verify this because sometimes these approximations can be tricky. Let me check the steps again. First, the harmonic number \( H_n = \sum_{k=1}^n 1/k \) is indeed asymptotic to \( \ln(n) + \gamma + 1/(2n) - 1/(12n^2) + \dots \). So, the difference \( H_n - \ln(n) \) approaches \( \gamma \) as \( n \to \infty \). Therefore, the ratio \( H_n / \ln(n) = 1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \). So, the term inside the limit is \( (1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) )^{-\ln(n)} \). Let me consider the logarithm of this term: \( -\ln(n) \cdot \ln\left(1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \right) \). Using the expansion \( \ln(1 + x) \approx x - x^2/2 + x^3/3 - \dots \) when \( x \) is small. Here, \( x = \gamma / \ln(n) \), which tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \). Therefore, \( \ln\left(1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \right) \approx \gamma / \ln(n) - (\gamma^2)/(2 (\ln(n))^2) + o(1/(\ln(n))^2) \). Multiplying this by \( -\ln(n) \): \( -\ln(n) \cdot \left( \gamma / \ln(n) - \gamma^2/(2 (\ln(n))^2) + o(1/(\ln(n))^2) \right ) \approx -\gamma + \gamma^2/(2 \ln(n)) + o(1/\ln(n)) \). Therefore, as \( n \to \infty \), this expression tends to \( -\gamma \), so \( \ln(L) = -\gamma \), hence \( L = e^{-\gamma} \). Therefore, the limit is \( e^{-\gamma} \). But let me check if there's another approach. Maybe using the known limit \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \left( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} - \ln(n) \right ) = \gamma \). Therefore, \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} = \ln(n) + \gamma + o(1) \). Therefore, the ratio \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} / \ln(n) = 1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) \). So the expression inside the limit is \( (1 + \gamma / \ln(n) + o(1/\ln(n)) )^{-\ln(n)} \). This is similar to the standard limit \( \lim_{x \to 0} (1 + a x)^{1/x} = e^a \). Here, if we set \( x = 1/\ln(n) \), then as \( n \to \infty \), \( x \to 0 \). Then, the expression becomes \( (1 + \gamma x + o(x))^{-1/x} \). Taking the logarithm of this expression: \( (-1/x) \cdot \ln(1 + \gamma x + o(x)) \approx (-1/x) \cdot ( \gamma x - (\gamma x)^2 / 2 + o(x) ) = -\gamma + \gamma^2 x / 2 + o(1) \). As \( x \to 0 \), this tends to \( -\gamma \), so the limit is \( e^{-\gamma} \). Yep, same result. Therefore, I feel confident that the answer is \( e^{-\gamma} \). But wait, just to make sure, let me compute the logarithm again step by step: Let’s denote \( a_n = \frac{H_n}{\ln(n)} = 1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} + o\left(\frac{1}{\ln(n)}\right) \). Then, \( \ln(a_n) = \ln\left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} + o\left(\frac{1}{\ln(n)}\right)\right) \approx \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} + o\left(\frac{1}{(\ln(n))^2}\right) \). Multiply by \( -\ln(n) \): \( -\ln(n) \cdot \ln(a_n) \approx -\ln(n) \cdot \left( \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} \right ) = -\gamma + \frac{\gamma^2}{2 \ln(n)} \). As \( n \to \infty \), the second term goes to 0, so the limit is \( -\gamma \), hence the original limit is \( e^{-\gamma} \). Therefore, the answer should be \( \boxed{e^{-\gamma}} \). **Final Answer** The limit is equal to \boxed{e^{-\gamma}}. </think> To evaluate the limit \( \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} \), we start by noting that the harmonic series \( \sum_{k=1}^n \frac{1}{k} \) is approximately \( \ln(n) + \gamma \) as \( n \) approaches infinity, where \( \gamma \) is the Euler-Mascheroni constant. Therefore, the ratio inside the parentheses can be approximated as \( 1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \). We need to evaluate the limit of \( \left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right)^{-\ln(n)} \). Taking the natural logarithm of this expression, we get: \[ \ln\left( \left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right)^{-\ln(n)} \right) = -\ln(n) \cdot \ln\left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right) \] Using the expansion \( \ln(1 + x) \approx x - \frac{x^2}{2} + \cdots \) for small \( x \), we substitute \( x = \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} \): \[ \ln\left(1 + \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)}\right) \approx \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} \] Multiplying by \( -\ln(n) \): \[ -\ln(n) \left( \frac{\gamma}{\ln(n)} - \frac{\gamma^2}{2 (\ln(n))^2} \right) = -\gamma + \frac{\gamma^2}{2 \ln(n)} \] As \( n \to \infty \), the term \( \frac{\gamma^2}{2 \ln(n)} \) approaches 0, leaving us with \( -\gamma \). Exponentiating this result gives the final limit: \[ \lim_{n\to\infty} \left(\frac{\sum^n_{k=1} \frac{1}{k}} { \ln(n) }\right)^{- \ln(n)} = e^{-\gamma} \] Thus, the limit is \(\boxed{e^{-\gamma}}\).
